</tbody></table><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1220493105">html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota tr{background:transparent!important}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota img{background:white}@media(prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota tr{background:transparent!important}html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota img{background:white}}</style>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Citrullus_lanatus_var._citroides.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Citrullus_lanatus_var._citroides.JPG/220px-Citrullus_lanatus_var._citroides.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="209" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Citrullus_lanatus_var._citroides.JPG/330px-Citrullus_lanatus_var._citroides.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Citrullus_lanatus_var._citroides.JPG/440px-Citrullus_lanatus_var._citroides.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1077" data-file-height="1023" /></a><figcaption>A <i><a href="/wiki/Citron_melon" title="Citron melon">tsamma</a></i> in the <a href="/wiki/Kalahari_Desert" title="Kalahari Desert">Kalahari Desert</a></figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Citrullus_lanatus_afghan_melon.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Citrullus_lanatus_afghan_melon.jpg/220px-Citrullus_lanatus_afghan_melon.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="106" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Citrullus_lanatus_afghan_melon.jpg/330px-Citrullus_lanatus_afghan_melon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Citrullus_lanatus_afghan_melon.jpg/440px-Citrullus_lanatus_afghan_melon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4060" data-file-height="1952" /></a><figcaption>Naturalized in Australia</figcaption></figure>
<p><b>Watermelon</b> (<i>Citrullus lanatus</i>) is a <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">flowering plant</a> species of the <a href="/wiki/Cucurbitaceae" title="Cucurbitaceae">Cucurbitaceae</a> family and the name of its <a href="/wiki/Edible_fruit" class="mw-redirect" title="Edible fruit">edible fruit</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_botanical_terms#scandent" title="Glossary of botanical terms">scrambling and trailing</a> <a href="/wiki/Vine" title="Vine">vine</a>-like plant, it is a <a href="/wiki/Plant_breeding" title="Plant breeding">highly cultivated</a> fruit worldwide, with more than 1,000 <a href="/wiki/Variety_(botany)" title="Variety (botany)">varieties</a>.
</p><p>Watermelon is grown in favorable <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climates</a> from <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropical</a> to <a href="/wiki/Temperate_climate" title="Temperate climate">temperate</a> regions worldwide for its large edible <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruit</a>, which is a <a href="/wiki/Berry_(botany)" title="Berry (botany)">berry</a> with a hard rind and no internal divisions, and is <a href="/wiki/Botany" title="Botany">botanically</a> called a <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_botanical_terms#pepo" title="Glossary of botanical terms"><i>pepo</i></a>. The sweet, juicy flesh is usually deep red to pink, with many black seeds, although <a href="/wiki/Seedless_fruit" title="Seedless fruit">seedless</a> varieties exist. The fruit can be eaten raw or <a href="/wiki/Pickled" class="mw-redirect" title="Pickled">pickled</a>, and the rind is edible after cooking. It may also be consumed as a juice or an ingredient in mixed beverages.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kordofan" title="Kordofan">Kordofan</a> melons from <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> are the closest relatives and may be <a href="/wiki/Progenitor" title="Progenitor">progenitors</a> of modern, cultivated watermelons.<sup id="cite_ref-renner21_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-renner21-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Wild watermelon seeds were found in <a href="/wiki/Uan_Muhuggiag" title="Uan Muhuggiag">Uan Muhuggiag</a>, a prehistoric site in <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> that dates to approximately 3500&#160;BC.<sup id="cite_ref-VegHist_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VegHist-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> In 2022, a study was released that traced 6,000-year-old watermelon seeds found in the Libyan desert to the Egusi seeds of Nigeria, West Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Watermelons were domesticated in north-east Africa and cultivated in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> by 2000&#160;BC, although they were not the sweet modern variety. Sweet dessert watermelons spread across the Mediterranean world during <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman times</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Paris_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Paris-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Considerable <a href="/wiki/Plant_breeding" title="Plant breeding">breeding</a> effort has developed disease-resistant varieties. Many <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivars</a> are available that produce mature fruit within 100 days of planting. In 2017, China produced about two-thirds of the world's total of watermelons.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+link+.ambox{margin-top:-1px}html body.mediawiki .mw-parser-output .ambox.mbox-small-left{margin:4px 1em 4px 0;overflow:hidden;width:238px;border-collapse:collapse;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em}.mw-parser-output .ambox-speedy{border-left:10px solid #b32424;background-color:#fee7e6}.mw-parser-output .ambox-delete{border-left:10px solid #b32424}.mw-parser-output .ambox-content{border-left:10px solid #f28500}.mw-parser-output .ambox-style{border-left:10px solid #fc3}.mw-parser-output .ambox-move{border-left:10px solid #9932cc}.mw-parser-output .ambox-protection{border-left:10px solid #a2a9b1}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-text{border:none;padding:0.25em 0.5em;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-image{border:none;padding:2px 0 2px 0.5em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-imageright{border:none;padding:2px 0.5em 2px 0;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-empty-cell{border:none;padding:0;width:1px}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-image-div{width:52px}html.client-js body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></span></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Watermelon" title="Special:EditPage/Watermelon">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>&#32;in this section. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">January 2022</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The watermelon is an <a href="/wiki/Annual_plant" title="Annual plant">annual</a> that has a prostrate or climbing habit. Stems are up to 3 metres (10 feet) long and new growth has yellow or brown hairs. Leaves are <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1154941027">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);clip-path:polygon(0px 0px,0px 0px,0px 0px);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style>60 to 200 millimetres (<span class="frac">2<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">4</span></span> to <span class="frac">7<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">3</span>&#8260;<span class="den">4</span></span> inches) long and <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1154941027">40 to 150&#160;mm (<span class="frac">1<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">2</span></span> to 6&#160;in) wide. These usually have three lobes that are lobed or doubly lobed. Young growth is densely woolly with yellowish-brown hairs which disappear as the plant ages. Like all but one species in the genus <i>Citrullus</i>, watermelon has branching <a href="/wiki/Tendril" title="Tendril">tendrils</a>. Plants have unisexual male or female flowers that are white or yellow and borne on <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1154941027">40-millimetre-long (<span class="frac">1<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">2</span></span>&#160;in) hairy stalks. Each flower grows singly in the leaf axils, and the species' <a href="/wiki/Sexual_system" title="Sexual system">sexual system</a>, with male and female flowers produced on each plant, is <a href="/wiki/Monoecious" class="mw-redirect" title="Monoecious">monoecious</a>. The male flowers predominate at the beginning of the season; the female flowers, which develop later, have inferior ovaries. The <a href="/wiki/Style_(botany)" title="Style (botany)">styles</a> are united into a single column.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The large fruit is a kind of modified berry called a <i>pepo</i> with a thick <a href="/wiki/Peel_(fruit)" title="Peel (fruit)">rind</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pericarp" class="mw-redirect" title="Pericarp">exocarp</a>) and fleshy center (<a href="/wiki/Mesocarp" class="mw-redirect" title="Mesocarp">mesocarp</a> and endocarp).<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Wild plants have fruits up to 20&#160;cm (8&#160;in) in diameter, while cultivated varieties may exceed 60&#160;cm (24&#160;in). The rind of the fruit is mid- to dark green and usually mottled or striped, and the flesh, containing numerous <a href="/wiki/Seed" title="Seed">pips</a> spread throughout the inside, can be red or pink (most commonly), orange, yellow, green or white.<sup id="cite_ref-Maynard_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maynard-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SANBI_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SANBI-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A bitter watermelon, <i><a href="/wiki/Citron_melon" title="Citron melon">C. amarus</a></i>, has become <a href="/wiki/Naturalisation_(biology)" title="Naturalisation (biology)">naturalized</a> in semiarid regions of several continents, and is designated as a "pest plant" in parts of <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a> where they are called "pig melon".<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span></h2>
<p>The sweet watermelon was first described by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> in 1753 and given the name <i>Cucurbita citrullus</i>. It was reassigned to the genus <i>Citrullus</i> in 1836, under the <a href="/wiki/Replacement_name" class="mw-redirect" title="Replacement name">replacement name</a> <i>Citrullus vulgaris</i>, by the German botanist <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Adolf_Schrader" class="mw-redirect" title="Heinrich Adolf Schrader">Heinrich Adolf Schrader</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IPNI_291943-1_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPNI_291943-1-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> (The <a href="/wiki/International_Code_of_Nomenclature_for_algae,_fungi,_and_plants" title="International Code of Nomenclature for algae, fungi, and plants">International Code of Nomenclature for algae, fungi, and plants</a> does not allow names like "<i>Citrullus citrullus</i>".)<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The species is further divided into several varieties, of which bitter wooly melon (<i>Citrullus lanatus</i> (Thunb.) Matsum. &amp; Nakai var. <i>lanatus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Citron_melon" title="Citron melon">citron melons</a> (<i>Citrullus lanatus</i> var. <i>citroides</i> (L. H. Bailey) Mansf.), and the edible var. <i>vulgaris</i> may be the most important. This taxonomy originated with the erroneous synonymization of the wooly melon <i>Citrullus lanatus</i> with the sweet watermelon <i>Citrullus vulgaris</i> by <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Hyde_Bailey" title="Liberty Hyde Bailey">L.H. Bailey</a> in 1930.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Molecular data, including sequences from the original collection of Thunberg and other relevant type material, show that the sweet watermelon (<i>Citrullus vulgaris</i> Schrad.) and the bitter wooly melon <i>Citrullus lanatus</i> (Thunb.) Matsum. &amp; Nakai are not closely related to each other.<sup id="cite_ref-Chomicki_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chomicki-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> A proposal to conserve the name, <i>Citrullus lanatus</i> (Thunb.) Matsum. &amp; Nakai, was accepted by the <a href="/wiki/International_Code_of_Nomenclature_for_algae,_fungi,_and_plants" title="International Code of Nomenclature for algae, fungi, and plants">nomenclature</a> committee and confirmed at the <a href="/wiki/International_Botanical_Congress" title="International Botanical Congress">International Botanical Congress</a> in 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-Renner14_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Renner14-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Prior to 2015, the wild species closest to <i>Citrullus lanatus</i> was assumed to be the tendril-less melon <i><a href="/wiki/Citrullus_ecirrhosus" title="Citrullus ecirrhosus">Citrullus ecirrhosus</a></i> Cogn. from South African arid regions based on an erroneously identified 18th-century specimen. However, after phylogenetic analysis, the closest relative to <i>Citrullus lanatus</i> is now thought to be <i>Citrullus mucosospermus</i> (Fursa) from West Africa (from Senegal to Nigeria), which is also sometimes considered a subspecies within <i>C. lanatus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Watermelon populations from Sudan are also close to domesticated watermelons.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The bitter wooly melon was formally described by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Peter_Thunberg" title="Carl Peter Thunberg">Carl Peter Thunberg</a> in 1794 and given the name <i>Momordica lanata</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-APNI1_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-APNI1-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> It was reassigned to the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Citrullus" title="Citrullus">Citrullus</a></i> in 1916 by Japanese botanists <a href="/wiki/Jinz%C5%8D_Matsumura" title="Jinzō Matsumura">Jinzō Matsumura</a> and <a href="/wiki/Takenoshin_Nakai" title="Takenoshin Nakai">Takenoshin Nakai</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-APNI2_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-APNI2-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Albert_Eckhout_1610-1666_Brazilian_fruits.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Albert_Eckhout_1610-1666_Brazilian_fruits.jpg/250px-Albert_Eckhout_1610-1666_Brazilian_fruits.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="253" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Albert_Eckhout_1610-1666_Brazilian_fruits.jpg/375px-Albert_Eckhout_1610-1666_Brazilian_fruits.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Albert_Eckhout_1610-1666_Brazilian_fruits.jpg/500px-Albert_Eckhout_1610-1666_Brazilian_fruits.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1800" data-file-height="1821" /></a><figcaption><i>Still Life with Watermelons, Pineapple and Other Fruit</i> by <a href="/wiki/Albert_Eckhout" title="Albert Eckhout">Albert Eckhout</a>, a Dutch painter active in 17th-century Brazil</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Leiden_University_Library_-_Seikei_Zusetsu_vol._27,_page_018_-_%E8%A5%BF%E7%93%9C_-_Citrullus_lanatus_(Thunb.)_Matsum._%26_Nakai_-_Cucurbita_maxima_Duchesne,_1804.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Leiden_University_Library_-_Seikei_Zusetsu_vol._27%2C_page_018_-_%E8%A5%BF%E7%93%9C_-_Citrullus_lanatus_%28Thunb.%29_Matsum._%26_Nakai_-_Cucurbita_maxima_Duchesne%2C_1804.jpg/220px-Leiden_University_Library_-_Seikei_Zusetsu_vol._27%2C_page_018_-_%E8%A5%BF%E7%93%9C_-_Citrullus_lanatus_%28Thunb.%29_Matsum._%26_Nakai_-_Cucurbita_maxima_Duchesne%2C_1804.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="172" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Leiden_University_Library_-_Seikei_Zusetsu_vol._27%2C_page_018_-_%E8%A5%BF%E7%93%9C_-_Citrullus_lanatus_%28Thunb.%29_Matsum._%26_Nakai_-_Cucurbita_maxima_Duchesne%2C_1804.jpg/330px-Leiden_University_Library_-_Seikei_Zusetsu_vol._27%2C_page_018_-_%E8%A5%BF%E7%93%9C_-_Citrullus_lanatus_%28Thunb.%29_Matsum._%26_Nakai_-_Cucurbita_maxima_Duchesne%2C_1804.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Leiden_University_Library_-_Seikei_Zusetsu_vol._27%2C_page_018_-_%E8%A5%BF%E7%93%9C_-_Citrullus_lanatus_%28Thunb.%29_Matsum._%26_Nakai_-_Cucurbita_maxima_Duchesne%2C_1804.jpg/440px-Leiden_University_Library_-_Seikei_Zusetsu_vol._27%2C_page_018_-_%E8%A5%BF%E7%93%9C_-_Citrullus_lanatus_%28Thunb.%29_Matsum._%26_Nakai_-_Cucurbita_maxima_Duchesne%2C_1804.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="468" /></a><figcaption>Illustration from the Japanese agricultural encyclopedia Seikei Zusetsu (1804)</figcaption></figure>
<p>Watermelons were originally cultivated for their high water content and stored to be eaten during dry seasons, as a source of both food and water.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Watermelon seeds were found in the <a href="/wiki/Dead_Sea" title="Dead Sea">Dead Sea</a> region at the ancient settlements of <a href="/wiki/Bab_edh-Dhra" title="Bab edh-Dhra">Bab edh-Dhra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tel_Arad" title="Tel Arad">Tel Arad</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many 5000-year-old wild watermelon seeds (<i>C. lanatus</i>) were discovered at <a href="/wiki/Uan_Muhuggiag" title="Uan Muhuggiag">Uan Muhuggiag</a>, a prehistoric archaeological site located in southwestern <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>. This archaeobotanical discovery may support the possibility that the plant was more widely distributed in the past.<sup id="cite_ref-VegHist_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VegHist-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/7th_century" title="7th century">7th century</a>, watermelons were being cultivated in India, and by the 10th century had reached China. The <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">Moors</a> introduced the fruit into the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>, and there is evidence of it being cultivated in <a href="/wiki/C%C3%B3rdoba,_Spain" title="Córdoba, Spain">Córdoba</a> in 961 and also in <a href="/wiki/Seville" title="Seville">Seville</a> in 1158. It spread northwards through <a href="/wiki/Southern_Europe" title="Southern Europe">southern Europe</a>, perhaps limited in its advance by summer temperatures being insufficient for good yields. The fruit had begun appearing in European <a href="/wiki/Herbal" title="Herbal">herbals</a> by 1600, and was widely planted in Europe in the 17th century as a minor garden crop.<sup id="cite_ref-Maynard_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maynard-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Early watermelons were not sweet, but bitter, with yellowish-white flesh. They were also difficult to open. The modern watermelon, which tastes sweeter and is easier to open, was developed over time through <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">selective breeding</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>European colonists and enslaved people from Africa introduced the watermelon to the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>. <a href="/wiki/Spanish_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Spanish colonization of the Americas">Spanish settlers</a> were growing it in Florida in 1576. It was being grown in Massachusetts by 1629, and by 1650 was being cultivated in <a href="/wiki/Viceroyalty_of_Peru" title="Viceroyalty of Peru">Peru</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Brazil" title="Colonial Brazil">Brazil</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Panama" title="History of Panama">Panama</a>. Around the same time, <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native Americans</a> were cultivating the crop in the Mississippi valley and <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>. Watermelons were rapidly accepted in <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Pacific_islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Pacific islands">Pacific islands</a> when they were introduced there by explorers such as <a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">Captain James Cook</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Maynard_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maynard-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War era</a> United States, watermelons were commonly grown by free <a href="/wiki/Black_people" title="Black people">black people</a> and became one symbol for the abolition of slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> After the Civil War, black people were maligned for their association with watermelon. The sentiment evolved into a racist <a href="/wiki/Watermelon_stereotype" title="Watermelon stereotype">stereotype</a> where black people shared a supposed voracious appetite for watermelon, a fruit long associated with laziness and uncleanliness.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Seedless watermelons were initially developed in 1939 by Japanese scientists who were able to create seedless <a href="/wiki/Polyploid" class="mw-redirect" title="Polyploid">triploid</a> <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybrids</a> which remained rare initially because they did not have sufficient <a href="/wiki/Disease_resistance" title="Disease resistance">disease resistance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Seedless watermelons became more popular in the 21st century, rising to nearly 85% of total watermelon sales in the United States in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Systematics">Systematics</span></h2>
<p>A melon from the <a href="/wiki/Kordofan" title="Kordofan">Kordofan</a> region of <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> – the <i>kordofan melon</i> – may be the <a href="/wiki/Progenitor" title="Progenitor">progenitor</a> of the modern, domesticated watermelon.<sup id="cite_ref-renner21_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-renner21-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The kordofan melon shares with the domestic watermelon loss of the bitterness gene while maintaining a sweet taste, unlike other wild African varieties from other regions, indicating a common origin, possibly cultivated in the Nile Valley by 2340 BC.<sup id="cite_ref-renner21_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-renner21-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">Composition</span></h2>

</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1218072481"></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;font-weight:normal;padding-right:0.25em;"><b style="margin-left:-0.65em">Other constituents</b></th><td class="infobox-data" style="vertical-align:middle;padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;"><b>Quantity</b></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;font-weight:normal;padding-right:0.25em;">Water</th><td class="infobox-data" style="vertical-align:middle;padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;">91.45&#160;g</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;font-weight:normal;padding-right:0.25em;"><a href="/wiki/Lycopene" title="Lycopene">Lycopene</a></th><td class="infobox-data" style="vertical-align:middle;padding-left:0.65em;line-height:1.1em;">4532 μg</td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data"><hr /><div class="wrap" style="padding:0.3em;line-height:1.2em;"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://fdc.nal.usda.gov/fdc-app.html#/food-details/167765/nutrients">Link to USDA Database entry</a></div></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-below wrap" style="background:#e0e0e0;padding:0.3em;line-height:1.5em;font-weight:normal;"><sup>†</sup>Percentages estimated using <a href="/wiki/Reference_Daily_Intake#Daily_Values" title="Reference Daily Intake">US&#160;recommendations</a> for adults,<sup id="cite_ref-FDADailyValues_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FDADailyValues-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> except for potassium, which is estimated based on expert recommendation from <a href="/wiki/National_Academies_of_Sciences,_Engineering,_and_Medicine" title="National Academies of Sciences, Engineering, and Medicine">the National Academies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NationalAcademiesPotassium_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NationalAcademiesPotassium-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Watermelon fruit is 91% <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>, contains 6% sugars, and is low in <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a> (table).<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1154941027">100-gram (<span class="frac">3<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">2</span></span>-ounce) serving, watermelon fruit supplies 125 kilojoules (30 kilocalories) of food energy and low amounts of <a href="/wiki/Essential_nutrients" class="mw-redirect" title="Essential nutrients">essential nutrients</a> (see table). Only <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">vitamin C</a> is present in appreciable content at 10% of the <a href="/wiki/Daily_Value" class="mw-redirect" title="Daily Value">Daily Value</a> (table). Watermelon pulp contains <a href="/wiki/Carotenoids" class="mw-redirect" title="Carotenoids">carotenoids</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Lycopene" title="Lycopene">lycopene</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acid</a> <a href="/wiki/Citrulline" title="Citrulline">citrulline</a> is produced in watermelon <a href="/wiki/Peel_(fruit)" title="Peel (fruit)">rind</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Varieties">Varieties</span></h2>
<p>A number of cultivar groups have been identified:<sup id="cite_ref-MMPND_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MMPND-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Citroides_group"><i>Citroides</i> group</span></h3>
<p>(syn.  <i>C. lanatus</i> subsp. <i>lanatus</i> var. <i>citroides</i>;  <i>C. lanatus</i> var. <i>citroides</i>; <i>C. vulgaris</i> var. <i>citroides</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-MMPND_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MMPND-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>DNA data reveal that <i>C. lanatus</i> var. <i>citroides</i> Bailey is the same as Thunberg's bitter wooly melon, <i>C. lanatus</i> and also the same as <i>C. amarus</i> Schrad.  It is not a form of the sweet watermelon <i>C. vulgaris</i> nor closely related to that species.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Citron_melon" title="Citron melon">citron melon</a> or <i>makataan</i> – a variety with sweet yellow flesh that is cultivated around the world for fodder and the production of citron peel and <a href="/wiki/Pectin" title="Pectin">pectin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-plantzafrica_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plantzafrica-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lanatus_group"><i>Lanatus</i> group</span></h3>
<p>(syn. <i>C. lanatus</i> var. <i>caffer</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-MMPND_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MMPND-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>C. caffer</i> Schrad. is a synonym of <i>C. amarus</i> Schrad.
</p><p>The variety known as <i>tsamma</i> is grown for its juicy white flesh. The variety was an important food source for travellers in the <a href="/wiki/Kalahari_Desert" title="Kalahari Desert">Kalahari Desert</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-plantzafrica_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plantzafrica-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another variety known as <i>karkoer</i> or <i>bitterboela</i> is unpalatable to humans, but the seeds may be eaten.<sup id="cite_ref-plantzafrica_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plantzafrica-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A small-fruited form with a bumpy skin has caused poisoning in sheep.<sup id="cite_ref-plantzafrica_33-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plantzafrica-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vulgaris_group"><i>Vulgaris</i> group</span></h3>
<p>This is Linnaeus's sweet watermelon; it has been grown for human consumption for thousands of years.<sup id="cite_ref-plantzafrica_33-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plantzafrica-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i>C. lanatus</i> <i>mucosospermus</i> (Fursa) Fursa</li></ul>
<p>This West African species is the closest wild relative of the watermelon. It is cultivated for cattle feed.<sup id="cite_ref-plantzafrica_33-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-plantzafrica-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Additionally, other wild species have bitter fruit containing <a href="/wiki/Cucurbitacin" title="Cucurbitacin">cucurbitacin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-kew_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kew-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
<i>C. colocynthis</i> (L.) Schrad. ex Eckl. &amp; Zeyh.,
<i>C. rehmii</i> De Winter, and
<i>C. naudinianus</i> (Sond.) Hook.f.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Varieties_2">Varieties</span></h3>
<p>The more than 1,200<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivars</a> of watermelon range in weight from less than <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1154941027">1 kilogram (<span class="frac">2<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">4</span></span> pounds) to more than 90&#160;kg (200&#160;lb); the flesh can be red, pink, orange, yellow or white.<sup id="cite_ref-WSU_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSU-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>The 'Carolina Cross' produced the current world record for heaviest watermelon, weighing 159&#160;kg (351&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-weight_record_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weight_record-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> It has green skin, red flesh and commonly produces fruit between 29 and 68&#160;kg (65 and 150&#160;lb). It takes about 90 days from planting to harvest.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The 'Golden Midget' has a golden rind and pink flesh when ripe, and takes 70 days from planting to harvest.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The 'Orangeglo' has a very sweet orange flesh, and is a large, oblong fruit weighing 9–14&#160;kg (20–31&#160;lb). It has a light green rind with jagged dark green stripes. It takes about 90–100 days from planting to harvest.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The 'Moon and Stars' variety was created in 1926.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The rind is purple/black and has many small yellow circles (stars) and one or two large yellow circles (moon). The melon weighs 9–23&#160;kg (20–51&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The flesh is pink or red and has brown seeds. The foliage is also spotted. The time from planting to harvest is about 90 days.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The 'Cream of Saskatchewan' has small, round fruits about 25&#160;cm (10&#160;in) in diameter. It has a thin, light and dark green striped rind, and sweet white flesh with black seeds. It can grow well in cool climates. It was originally brought to <a href="/wiki/Saskatchewan" title="Saskatchewan">Saskatchewan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Russian_Canadians" title="Russian Canadians">Russian immigrants</a>. The melon takes 80–85 days from planting to harvest.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The '<a href="/wiki/Melitopol" title="Melitopol">Melitopolski</a>' has small, round fruits roughly 28–30&#160;cm (11–12&#160;in) in diameter. It is an early ripening variety that originated from the <a href="/wiki/Astrakhan_Oblast" title="Astrakhan Oblast">Astrakhan</a> region of <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, an area known for cultivation of watermelons. The Melitopolski watermelons are seen piled high by vendors in <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a> in the summer. This variety takes around 95 days from planting to harvest.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The 'Densuke' watermelon has round fruit up to 11&#160;kg (24&#160;lb). The rind is black with no stripes or spots. It is grown only on the island of Hokkaido, Japan, where up to 10,000 watermelons are produced every year. In June 2008, one of the first harvested watermelons was sold at an auction for 650,000 yen (US$6,300), making it the most expensive watermelon ever sold. The average selling price is generally around 25,000 yen ($250).<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Many cultivars are no longer grown commercially because of their thick rind, but seeds may be available among home gardeners and specialty seed companies. This thick rind is desirable for making watermelon pickles, and some old cultivars favoured for this purpose include 'Tom Watson', 'Georgia Rattlesnake', and 'Black Diamond'.<sup id="cite_ref-Wehner2008_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wehner2008-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Pasteques,_extrait_d%27un_tableau_de_Giovanni_Stanchi.jpeg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Pasteques%2C_extrait_d%27un_tableau_de_Giovanni_Stanchi.jpeg/220px-Pasteques%2C_extrait_d%27un_tableau_de_Giovanni_Stanchi.jpeg" decoding="async" width="220" height="130" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Pasteques%2C_extrait_d%27un_tableau_de_Giovanni_Stanchi.jpeg/330px-Pasteques%2C_extrait_d%27un_tableau_de_Giovanni_Stanchi.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Pasteques%2C_extrait_d%27un_tableau_de_Giovanni_Stanchi.jpeg/440px-Pasteques%2C_extrait_d%27un_tableau_de_Giovanni_Stanchi.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="1684" data-file-height="998" /></a><figcaption>Watermelon (an old cultivar) as depicted in a 17th-century painting, oil on canvas, by <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Stanchi" title="Giovanni Stanchi">Giovanni Stanchi</a></figcaption></figure>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Variety_improvement">Variety improvement</span></h3>
<p>Charles Fredrick Andrus, a horticulturist at the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">USDA</a> Vegetable Breeding Laboratory in <a href="/wiki/Charleston,_South_Carolina" title="Charleston, South Carolina">Charleston, South Carolina</a>, set out to produce a disease-resistant and wilt-resistant watermelon. The result, in 1954, was "that gray melon from Charleston". Its oblong shape and hard rind made it easy to stack and ship. Its adaptability meant it could be grown over a wide geographical area. It produced high yields and was resistant to the most serious watermelon diseases: <a href="/wiki/Anthracnose" class="mw-redirect" title="Anthracnose">anthracnose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fusarium_wilt" title="Fusarium wilt">fusarium wilt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Others were also working on disease-resistant cultivars; J. M. Crall at the University of Florida produced 'Jubilee' in 1963 and C. V. Hall of Kansas State University produced 'Crimson Sweet' the following year. These are no longer grown to any great extent, but their lineage has been further developed into <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybrid</a> varieties with higher yields, better flesh quality and attractive appearance.<sup id="cite_ref-Maynard_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maynard-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Another objective of plant breeders has been the elimination of the seeds which occur scattered throughout the flesh. This has been achieved through the use of <a href="/wiki/Polyploid" class="mw-redirect" title="Polyploid">triploid</a> varieties, but these are sterile, and the cost of producing the seed by crossing a <a href="/wiki/Polyploid" class="mw-redirect" title="Polyploid">tetraploid</a> parent with a normal <a href="/wiki/Polyploid" class="mw-redirect" title="Polyploid">diploid</a> parent is high.<sup id="cite_ref-Maynard_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maynard-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2017, farmers in approximately 44 states in the United States grew watermelon commercially, producing more than $500 million worth of the fruit annually.<sup id="cite_ref-index_:_USDA_ARS_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-index_:_USDA_ARS-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Georgia, Florida, Texas, California and Arizona are the United States' largest watermelon producers, with Florida producing more watermelon than any other state.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-index_:_USDA_ARS_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-index_:_USDA_ARS-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> This now-common fruit is often large enough that groceries often sell half or quarter melons. Some smaller, spherical varieties of watermelon—both red- and yellow-fleshed—are sometimes called "icebox melons".<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The largest recorded fruit was grown in <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a> in 2013 and weighed 159 kilograms (351 pounds).<sup id="cite_ref-weight_record_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weight_record-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Culinary">Culinary</span></h3>
<p>Watermelon is a sweet, commonly consumed fruit of summer, usually as fresh slices, diced in mixed <a href="/wiki/Fruit_salad" title="Fruit salad">fruit salads</a>, or as juice.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Watermelon juice can be blended with other fruit juices or made into <a href="/wiki/Wine" title="Wine">wine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The seeds have a nutty flavor and can be dried and roasted, or ground into flour.<sup id="cite_ref-SANBI_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SANBI-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Watermelon rinds may be eaten, but their unappealing flavor may be overcome by <a href="/wiki/Pickling" title="Pickling">pickling</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Wehner2008_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wehner2008-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> sometimes eaten as a <a href="/wiki/Vegetable" title="Vegetable">vegetable</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stir_frying" title="Stir frying">stir-fried</a> or <a href="/wiki/Stew" title="Stew">stewed</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SANBI_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SANBI-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Citrullis lanatus</i>, variety <i>caffer</i>, grows wild in the <a href="/wiki/Kalahari_Desert" title="Kalahari Desert">Kalahari Desert</a>, where it is known as <a href="/wiki/Citron_melon" title="Citron melon">tsamma</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SANBI_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SANBI-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The fruits are used by the <a href="/wiki/San_people" title="San people">San people</a> and wild animals for both water and nourishment, allowing survival on a diet of tsamma for six weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-SANBI_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SANBI-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbolic">Symbolic</span></h3>
<p>The watermelon is used variously as a symbol of <a href="/wiki/Watermelon_(Palestinian_symbol)" title="Watermelon (Palestinian symbol)">Palestinian resistance</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kherson_watermelon" title="Kherson watermelon">Kherson</a> region in <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Eco-socialism#Ideology" title="Eco-socialism">Eco-socialism</a>. It has also been used as a <a href="/wiki/Watermelon_stereotype" title="Watermelon stereotype">racist stereotype</a> in the United States. 
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultivation">Cultivation</span></h2>
<p>Watermelons are plants grown from tropical to temperate climates, needing temperatures higher than about 25&#160;°C (77&#160;°F) to thrive. On a garden scale, seeds are usually sown in pots under cover and transplanted into the ground. Ideal conditions are a well-drained sandy loam with a pH between 5.7 and 7.2.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Major pests of the watermelon include <a href="/wiki/Aphid" title="Aphid">aphids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drosophila" title="Drosophila">fruit flies</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Root-knot_nematode" title="Root-knot nematode">root-knot nematodes</a>. In conditions of high humidity, the plants are prone to <a href="/wiki/Plant_pathology" title="Plant pathology">plant diseases</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Powdery_mildew" title="Powdery mildew">powdery mildew</a> and <a href="/wiki/Squash_mosaic_virus" title="Squash mosaic virus">mosaic virus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RHS_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RHS-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Some varieties often grown in Japan and other parts of the <a href="/wiki/Far_East" title="Far East">Far East</a> are susceptible to <a href="/wiki/Fusarium_wilt" title="Fusarium wilt">fusarium wilt</a>. <a href="/wiki/Grafting" title="Grafting">Grafting</a> such varieties onto disease-resistant <a href="/wiki/Rootstock" title="Rootstock">rootstocks</a> offers protection.<sup id="cite_ref-Maynard_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maynard-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Watermelon_seedless.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Watermelon_seedless.jpg/220px-Watermelon_seedless.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="132" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Watermelon_seedless.jpg/330px-Watermelon_seedless.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Watermelon_seedless.jpg/440px-Watermelon_seedless.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1377" data-file-height="828" /></a><figcaption>Seedless watermelon</figcaption></figure>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/US_Department_of_Agriculture" class="mw-redirect" title="US Department of Agriculture">US Department of Agriculture</a> recommends using at least one <a href="/wiki/Beehive" title="Beehive">beehive</a> per acre (4,000&#160;m<sup>2</sup> per hive) for <a href="/wiki/Pollination" title="Pollination">pollination</a> of conventional, seeded varieties for commercial plantings. Seedless hybrids have sterile pollen. This requires planting <a href="/wiki/Pollinizer" class="mw-redirect" title="Pollinizer">pollinizer</a> rows of varieties with viable pollen. Since the supply of viable pollen is reduced, and pollination is much more critical in producing the <a href="/wiki/Seedless" class="mw-redirect" title="Seedless">seedless</a> variety, the recommended number of hives per acre increases to three hives per acre (<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1154941027">1,300&#160;m<sup>2</sup> per hive). Watermelons have a longer growing period than other melons and can often take 85 days or more from the time of transplanting for the fruit to mature.<sup id="cite_ref-WSU_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSU-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Lack of pollen is thought to contribute to "hollow heart" which causes the flesh of the watermelon to develop a large hole, sometimes in an intricate, symmetric shape. Watermelons suffering from hollow heart are safe to consume.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Farmers of the <a href="/wiki/Zentsuji" class="mw-redirect" title="Zentsuji">Zentsuji</a> region of Japan found a way to grow <a href="/wiki/Cube" title="Cube">cubic</a> watermelons by growing the fruits in metal and glass boxes and making them assume the shape of the receptacle.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The cubic shape was originally designed to make the melons easier to stack and store, but these "<a href="/wiki/Square_watermelon" title="Square watermelon">square watermelons</a>" may be triple the price of normal ones, so appeal mainly to wealthy urban consumers.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Pyramid-shaped watermelons have also been developed, and any <a href="/wiki/Polyhedron" title="Polyhedron">polyhedral</a> shape may potentially be used.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Watermelons, which are called <span title="Khoisan languages collective text"><i lang="khi">tsamma</i></span> in <a href="/wiki/Khoisan_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Khoisan language">Khoisan language</a> and <span title="Tswana-language text"><i lang="tn">makataan</i></span> in <a href="/wiki/Tswana_language" title="Tswana language">Tswana language</a>, are important water sources in <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kalahari_desert" class="mw-redirect" title="Kalahari desert">Kalahari desert</a>, and <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a> for both humans and animals.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span></h3>
<p>In 2020, global production of watermelons was 101.6 million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>, with China (mainland) accounting for 60% of the total (60.1 million tonnes).<sup id="cite_ref-fao21_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fao21-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Secondary producers included <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> – all having annual production of 2-3 million tonnes in 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-fao21_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fao21-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
